<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Mountain range in Asia</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about a mountain range. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Himalaya_(disambiguation)" title="Himalaya (disambiguation)">Himalaya (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066479718">.mw-parser-output .infobox-subbox{padding:0;border:none;margin:-3px;width:auto;min-width:100%;font-size:100%;clear:none;float:none;background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .infobox-3cols-child{margin:auto}.mw-parser-output .infobox .navbar{font-size:100%}body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-header,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-subheader,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-above,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-title,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-image,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-full-data,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-below{text-align:center}</style><table class="infobox vcard" style="width:24.5em; <p>The <b>Himalayas</b>, or <b>Himalaya</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˌ/: secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="'h' in 'hi'">h</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/eɪ/: 'a' in 'face'">eɪ</span><span title="/./: syllable break">.</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span></span>,<span class="wrap"> </span><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'h' in 'hi'">h</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/ɑː/: 'a' in 'father'">ɑː</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="/j/: 'y' in 'yes'">j</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span></span>/</a></span></span>; <small>Sanskrit: </small><span class="IPA" lang="sa-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Sanskrit" title="Help:IPA/Sanskrit">[ɦɪmaːlɐjɐ]</a></span>; from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sanskrit_language" title="Sanskrit language">Sanskrit</a> <i> <span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">himá</i></span></i> 'snow, frost', and <i> <span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">ā-laya</i></span></i> 'dwelling, abode'),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED-Himalayan_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-OED-Himalayan-1">[1]</a></sup> is a <a href="/wiki/Mountain_range" title="Mountain range">mountain range</a> in Asia, separating the plains of the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Tibetan_Plateau" title="Tibetan Plateau">Tibetan Plateau</a>. The range has some of the planet's highest peaks, including the very highest, <a href="/wiki/Mount_Everest" title="Mount Everest">Mount Everest</a>. Over <a href="/wiki/List_of_highest_mountains_on_Earth" title="List of highest mountains on Earth">100 peaks</a> exceeding 7,200 m (23,600 ft) in elevation lie in the Himalayas. By contrast, the highest peak outside Asia (<a href="/wiki/Aconcagua" title="Aconcagua">Aconcagua</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Andes" title="Andes">Andes</a>) is 6,961 m (22,838 ft) tall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Himalayas abut or cross <a href="/wiki/Himalayan_states" title="Himalayan states">five countries</a>: <a href="/wiki/Bhutan" title="Bhutan">Bhutan</a>, India, <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a>, China, and Pakistan. The sovereignty of the range in the <a href="/wiki/Kashmir" title="Kashmir">Kashmir</a> region is disputed among India, Pakistan, and China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bishop-britannica_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-bishop-britannica-3">[3]</a></sup> The Himalayan range is bordered on the northwest by the <a href="/wiki/Karakoram" title="Karakoram">Karakoram</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hindu_Kush" title="Hindu Kush">Hindu Kush</a> ranges, on the north by the Tibetan Plateau, and on the south by the <a href="/wiki/Indo-Gangetic_Plain" title="Indo-Gangetic Plain">Indo-Gangetic Plain</a>. Some of the world's major <a href="/wiki/River" title="River">rivers</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Indus_River" title="Indus River">Indus</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ganges_river" title="Ganges river">Ganges</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yarlung_Tsangpo_River" title="Yarlung Tsangpo River">Tsangpo</a>–<a href="/wiki/Brahmaputra_River" title="Brahmaputra River">Brahmaputra</a>, rise in the vicinity of the Himalayas, and their combined <a href="/wiki/Drainage_basin" title="Drainage basin">drainage basin</a> is home to some 600 million people; 53 million people live in the Himalayas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DimriBookhagen2019_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-DimriBookhagen2019-4">[4]</a></sup> The Himalayas have profoundly shaped the cultures of <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">South Asia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tibet" title="Tibet">Tibet</a>. Many Himalayan peaks are sacred in <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>; the summits of several—<a href="/wiki/Kangchenjunga" title="Kangchenjunga">Kangchenjunga</a> (from the Indian side), <a href="/wiki/Gangkhar_Puensum" title="Gangkhar Puensum">Gangkhar Puensum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Machapuchare" title="Machapuchare">Machapuchare</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nanda_Devi" title="Nanda Devi">Nanda Devi</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kailas" title="Kailas">Kailas</a> in the Tibetan <a href="/wiki/Transhimalaya" title="Transhimalaya">Transhimalaya</a>—are off-limits to climbers.
</p><p>Lifted by the <a href="/wiki/Subduction" title="Subduction">subduction</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Plate" title="Indian Plate">Indian tectonic plate</a> under the <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_Plate" title="Eurasian Plate">Eurasian Plate</a>, the Himalayan mountain range runs west-northwest to east-southeast in an arc 2,400 km (1,500 mi) long.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> Its western anchor, <a href="/wiki/Nanga_Parbat" title="Nanga Parbat">Nanga Parbat</a>, lies just south of the northernmost bend of the Indus river. Its eastern anchor, <a href="/wiki/Namcha_Barwa" title="Namcha Barwa">Namcha Barwa</a>, lies immediately west of the great bend of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yarlung_Tsangpo_River" title="Yarlung Tsangpo River">Yarlung Tsangpo River</a>. The range varies in width from 350 km (220 mi) in the west to 150 km (93 mi) in the east.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.pl_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.pl-6">[6]</a></sup>
<span class="anchor" id="Etymology"></span>
</p>
<div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Name">Name</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Himalayas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Name">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The name of the range hails from the <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> <span title="Sanskrit-language text"><i lang="sa-Latn">Himālaya</i></span> (<span lang="sa"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E0%A4%B9%E0%A4%BF%E0%A4%AE%E0%A4%BE%E0%A4%B2%E0%A4%AF#Sanskrit" title="wikt:हिमालय">हिमालय</a></span> 'abode of the snow'<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>), from <span title="Sanskrit-language text"><i lang="sa-Latn">himá</i></span> (<span lang="sa"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E0%A4%B9%E0%A4%BF%E0%A4%AE#Sanskrit" title="wikt:हिम">हिम</a></span> 'snow'<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup>) and <span title="Sanskrit-language text"><i lang="sa-Latn">ā-laya</i></span> (<span lang="sa"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E0%A4%86%E0%A4%B2%E0%A4%AF#Sanskrit" title="wikt:आलय">आलय</a></span> 'home, dwelling'<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> They are now known as "<span class="nowrap">the Himalaya Mountains</span>", usually shortened to "the Himalayas".
</p><p>The mountains are known as the <span title="Nepali (macrolanguage)-language text"><i lang="ne-Latn">Himālaya</i></span> in <a href="/wiki/Nepali_language" title="Nepali language">Nepali</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hindi_language" title="Hindi language">Hindi</a> (both written <span title="Hindi-language text"><span lang="hi">हिमालय</span></span>), <i>Himāl</i> (हिमाल) in <a href="/wiki/Kumaoni_language" title="Kumaoni language">Kumaoni</a>, the <i>Himalaya</i> (<span title="Standard Tibetan-language text"><span lang="bo">ཧི་མ་ལ་ཡ་</span></span>) or 'The Land of Snow' (<span title="Standard Tibetan-language text"><span lang="bo">གངས་ཅན་ལྗོངས་</span></span>) in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_Tibetan" title="Standard Tibetan">Tibetan</a>, also known as <span title="Nepali (macrolanguage)-language text"><i lang="ne-Latn">Himālaya</i></span> in <a href="/wiki/Sinhala_language" title="Sinhala language">Sinhala</a> written as <span title="Sinhala-language text"><span lang="si">හිමාලය</span></span>, the <i>Himāliya</i> Mountain Range (<span title="Urdu-language text"><span lang="ur"><span class="Nastaliq" dir="rtl" style="font-family: 'Jameel Noori Nastaleeq', 'Urdu Typesetting', 'Noto Nastaliq Urdu', 'Noto Nastaliq Urdu Draft', 'Hussaini Nastaleeq', 'AlQalam Taj Nastaleeq', IranNastaliq, 'Awami Nastaliq', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta3', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta2', 'Awami Nastaliq Beta1', 'Nafees Nastaleeq', 'Nafees Nastaleeq v1.01', 'Pak Nastaleeq', 'PDMS_Jauhar', 'Alvi Lahori Nastaleeq'; font-size: 110%; font-style: normal;" title="Nastaliq">سلسلہ کوہ ہمالیہ</span></span></span>) in <a href="/wiki/Urdu" title="Urdu">Urdu</a>, the <i>Himaloy Parvatmala</i> (<span title="Bengali-language text"><span lang="bn">হিমালয় পর্বতমালা</span></span>) in <a href="/wiki/Bengali_language" title="Bengali language">Bengali</a> and the <i>Ximalaya</i> Mountain Range (<a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters">simplified Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hans">喜马拉雅<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%B1%B1%E8%84%89" title="wikt:山脉">山脉</a></span>; <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">traditional Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">喜馬拉雅<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%B1%B1%E8%84%89" title="wikt:山脉">山脉</a></span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">Xǐmǎlāyǎ Shānmài</span></i>) in <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>.
</p><p>The name of the range is sometimes also given as <a href="/wiki/Himavat" title="Himavat">Himavan</a> in older writings, including the Sanskrit epic <a href="/wiki/Mahabharata" title="Mahabharata">Mahabharata</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Himavat" title="Himavat">Himavat</a> (<a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a>: हिमवत्<i>)</i> or Himavan <b>Himavān</b> (<a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a>: हिमवान्) is a Hindu deity who is the personification of the Himalayan Mountain Range. Other epithets include <b>Himaraja</b> (<a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a>: हिमराज, lit. <i>king of snow</i>) or <b>Parvateshwara</b> (<a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a>: पर्वतेश्वर, lit. <i>lord of mountains</i>).
</p><p>In western literature, some writers refer to it as <i>the Himalaya</i>. This was also previously transcribed as <i>Himmaleh</i>, as in <a href="/wiki/Emily_Dickinson" title="Emily Dickinson">Emily Dickinson</a>'s poetry<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Henry_David_Thoreau" title="Henry David Thoreau">Henry David Thoreau</a>'s essays.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography_and_key_features">Geography and key features</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Himalayas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Geography and key features">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Himalayan_peaks_and_passes" title="List of Himalayan peaks and passes">List of Himalayan peaks and passes</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Himalayas_Map.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="539" data-file-width="761" decoding="async" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Himalayas_Map.png/220px-Himalayas_Map.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Himalayas_Map.png/330px-Himalayas_Map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Himalayas_Map.png/440px-Himalayas_Map.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Himalayas_Map.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of the Himalayas (including the <a href="/wiki/Hindu_Kush" title="Hindu Kush">Hindu Kush</a>)</div></div></div>
<p>The Himalayas consist of parallel <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mountain_ranges" title="Mountain ranges">mountain ranges</a>: the <a href="/wiki/Sivalik_Hills" title="Sivalik Hills">Sivalik Hills</a> on the south; the <a href="/wiki/Lower_Himalayan_Range" title="Lower Himalayan Range">Lower Himalayan Range</a>; the <a href="/wiki/Great_Himalayas" title="Great Himalayas">Great Himalayas</a>, which is the highest and central range; and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tibetan_Himalayas" title="Tibetan Himalayas">Tibetan Himalayas</a> on the north.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Karakoram" title="Karakoram">Karakoram</a> are generally considered separate from the Himalayas.
</p><p>In the middle of the great curve of the Himalayan mountains lie the 8,000 m (26,000 ft) peaks of <a href="/wiki/Dhaulagiri" title="Dhaulagiri">Dhaulagiri</a> and <a href="/wiki/Annapurna" title="Annapurna">Annapurna</a> in <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a>, separated by the <a href="/wiki/Kali_Gandaki_Gorge" title="Kali Gandaki Gorge">Kali Gandaki Gorge</a>. The gorge splits the Himalayas into Western and Eastern sections both ecologically and orographically – the pass at the head of the Kali Gandaki the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kora_La" title="Kora La">Kora La</a> is the lowest point on the ridgeline between Everest and <a href="/wiki/K2" title="K2">K2</a> (the highest peak of the Karakoram range). To the east of Annapurna are the 8,000 m (5.0 mi) peaks of <a href="/wiki/Manaslu" title="Manaslu">Manaslu</a> and across the border in Tibet, <a href="/wiki/Shishapangma" title="Shishapangma">Shishapangma</a>. To the south of these lies <a href="/wiki/Kathmandu" title="Kathmandu">Kathmandu</a>, the capital of Nepal and the largest city in the Himalayas. East of the <a href="/wiki/Kathmandu_Valley" title="Kathmandu Valley">Kathmandu Valley</a> lies the valley of the Bhote/<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sun_Kosi" title="Sun Kosi">Sun Kosi</a> river which rises in Tibet and provides the main overland route between Nepal and China – the <a href="/wiki/Araniko_Highway" title="Araniko Highway">Araniko Highway</a>/<a href="/wiki/China_National_Highway_318" title="China National Highway 318">China National Highway 318</a>. Further east is the <a href="/wiki/Mahalangur_Himal" title="Mahalangur Himal">Mahalangur Himal</a> with four of the world's six highest mountains, including the highest: <a href="/wiki/Cho_Oyu" title="Cho Oyu">Cho Oyu</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Everest" title="Everest">Everest</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lhotse" title="Lhotse">Lhotse</a> and <a href="/wiki/Makalu" title="Makalu">Makalu</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Khumbu" title="Khumbu">Khumbu</a> region, popular for trekking, is found here on the south-western approaches to Everest. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arun_River,_China-Nepal" title="Arun River, China-Nepal">Arun river</a> drains the northern slopes of these mountains, before turning south and flowing to the range to the east of Makalu.
</p><p>In the far east of Nepal, the Himalayas rise to the <a href="/wiki/Kangchenjunga" title="Kangchenjunga">Kangchenjunga</a> massif on the border with India, the third highest mountain in the world, the most easterly 8,000 m (26,000 ft) summit and the highest point of India. The eastern side of Kangchenjunga is in the Indian state of <a href="/wiki/Sikkim" title="Sikkim">Sikkim</a>. Formerly an independent Kingdom, it lies on the main route from India to <a href="/wiki/Lhasa" title="Lhasa">Lhasa</a>, Tibet, which passes over the <a href="/wiki/Nathu_La" title="Nathu La">Nathu La</a> pass into Tibet. East of Sikkim lies the ancient Buddhist Kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Bhutan" title="Bhutan">Bhutan</a>. The highest mountain in Bhutan is <a href="/wiki/Gangkhar_Puensum" title="Gangkhar Puensum">Gangkhar Puensum</a>, which is also a strong candidate for the highest unclimbed mountain in the world. The Himalayas here are becoming increasingly rugged with heavily forested steep valleys. The Himalayas continue, turning slightly northeast, through the Indian State of <a href="/wiki/Arunachal_Pradesh" title="Arunachal Pradesh">Arunachal Pradesh</a> as well as Tibet, before reaching their easterly conclusion in the peak of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Namche_Barwa" title="Namche Barwa">Namche Barwa</a>, situated in Tibet inside the great bend of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yarlung_Tsangpo_River" title="Yarlung Tsangpo River">Yarlang Tsangpo</a> river. On the other side of the Tsangpo, to the east, are the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kangri_Garpo" title="Kangri Garpo">Kangri Garpo</a> mountains. The high mountains to the north of the Tsangpo including <a href="/wiki/Gyala_Peri" title="Gyala Peri">Gyala Peri</a>, however, are also sometimes included in the Himalayas.
</p><p>Going west from Dhaulagiri, Western Nepal is somewhat remote and lacks major high mountains, but is home to <a href="/wiki/Rara_Lake" title="Rara Lake">Rara Lake</a>, the largest lake in Nepal. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Karnali_River" title="Karnali River">Karnali River</a> rises in Tibet but cuts through the centre of the region. Further west, the border with India follows the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sarda_River" title="Sarda River">Sarda River</a> and provides a trade route into China, where on the Tibetan plateau lies the high peak of <a href="/wiki/Gurla_Mandhata" title="Gurla Mandhata">Gurla Mandhata</a>. Just across <a href="/wiki/Lake_Manasarovar" title="Lake Manasarovar">Lake Manasarovar</a> from this lies the sacred <a href="/wiki/Mount_Kailash" title="Mount Kailash">Mount Kailash</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kailash_Ranges" title="Kailash Ranges">Kailash Ranges</a>, which stands close to the source of the four main rivers of Himalayas and is revered in Hinduism, Buddhism, Sufism, Jainism, and Bonpo. In <a href="/wiki/Uttarakhand" title="Uttarakhand">Uttarakhand</a>, the Himalayas rise again as the Kumaon and Garhwal Himalayas<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britannica_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-britannica-16">[16]</a></sup> with the high peaks of <a href="/wiki/Nanda_Devi" title="Nanda Devi">Nanda Devi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kamet" title="Kamet">Kamet</a>. The state is also home to the important pilgrimage destinations of <a href="/wiki/Chota_Char_Dham" title="Chota Char Dham">Chaar Dhaam</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Gangotri" title="Gangotri">Gangotri</a>, the source of the holy river <a href="/wiki/Ganges" title="Ganges">Ganges</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yamunotri" title="Yamunotri">Yamunotri</a>, the source of the river <a href="/wiki/Yamuna" title="Yamuna">Yamuna</a>, and the temples at <a href="/wiki/Badrinath" title="Badrinath">Badrinath</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kedarnath" title="Kedarnath">Kedarnath</a>. Uttarakhand Himalayas are regionally divided into two, namely, Kumaon hills in <a href="/wiki/Kumaon_division" title="Kumaon division">Kumaon division</a> and Garhwal hills in <a href="/wiki/Garhwal_division" title="Garhwal division">Garhwal division</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>The next Himalayan Indian state, <a href="/wiki/Himachal_Pradesh" title="Himachal Pradesh">Himachal Pradesh</a>, is noted for its hill stations, particularly <a href="/wiki/Shimla" title="Shimla">Shimla</a>, the summer capital of the <a href="/wiki/British_Raj" title="British Raj">British Raj</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dharamsala,_Himachal_Pradesh" title="Dharamsala, Himachal Pradesh">Dharamsala</a>, the centre of the Tibetan community and <a href="/wiki/Central_Tibetan_Administration" title="Central Tibetan Administration">government in exile</a> in India. This area marks the start of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Punjab_Himalaya" title="Punjab Himalaya">Punjab Himalaya</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sutlej_river" title="Sutlej river">Sutlej river</a>, the most easterly of the five tributaries of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indus" title="Indus">Indus</a>, cuts through the range here. Further west, the Himalayas form much of the disputed Indian-administered union territory of <a href="/wiki/Jammu_and_Kashmir_(union_territory)" title="Jammu and Kashmir (union territory)">Jammu and Kashmir</a> where lies the renowned <a href="/wiki/Kashmir_Valley" title="Kashmir Valley">Kashmir Valley</a> and the town and lakes of <a href="/wiki/Srinagar" title="Srinagar">Srinagar</a>. The Himalayas form most of the south-west portion of the disputed Indian-administered union territory of <a href="/wiki/Ladakh" title="Ladakh">Ladakh</a>. The twin peaks of <a href="/wiki/Nun_Kun" title="Nun Kun">Nun Kun</a> are the only mountains over 7,000 m (4.3 mi) in this part of the Himalayas. Finally, the Himalayas reach their western end in the dramatic 8000 m peak of <a href="/wiki/Nanga_Parbat" title="Nanga Parbat">Nanga Parbat</a>, which rises over 8,000 m (26,000 ft) above the Indus valley and is the most westerly of the 8000 m summits. The western end terminates at a magnificent point near Nanga Parbat where the Himalayas intersect with the Karakoram and <a href="/wiki/Hindu_Kush" title="Hindu Kush">Hindu Kush</a> ranges, in the disputed Pakistani-administered territory of <a href="/wiki/Gilgit-Baltistan" title="Gilgit-Baltistan">Gilgit-Baltistan</a>. Some portion of the Himalayas, such as the <a href="/wiki/Kaghan_Valley" title="Kaghan Valley">Kaghan Valley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Margalla_Hills" title="Margalla Hills">Margalla Hills</a> and <a href="/wiki/Galyat" title="Galyat">Galyat</a> tract, extend into the Pakistani provinces of <a href="/wiki/Khyber_Pakhtunkhwa" title="Khyber Pakhtunkhwa">Khyber Pakhtunkhwa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Punjab,_Pakistan" title="Punjab, Pakistan">Punjab</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geology">Geology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Himalayas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Geology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geology_of_the_Himalaya" title="Geology of the Himalaya">Geology of the Himalaya</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Himalaya-formation.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="424" data-file-width="234" decoding="async" height="308" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Himalaya-formation.gif/170px-Himalaya-formation.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/2f/Himalaya-formation.gif 1.5x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Himalaya-formation.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The 6,000-kilometre-plus (3,700 mi) journey of the India landmass (Indian Plate) before its collision with Asia (Eurasian Plate) about 40 to 50 million <a href="/wiki/Before_Present" title="Before Present">years ago</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGS_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-USGS-18">[18]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The Himalayan range is one of the youngest mountain ranges on the planet and consists mostly of uplifted <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sedimentary" title="Sedimentary">sedimentary</a> and <a href="/wiki/Metamorphic_rock" title="Metamorphic rock">metamorphic rock</a>. According to the modern theory of <a href="/wiki/Plate_tectonics" title="Plate tectonics">plate tectonics</a>, its formation is a result of a <a href="/wiki/Continental_collision" title="Continental collision">continental collision</a> or <a href="/wiki/Orogeny" title="Orogeny">orogeny</a> along the <a href="/wiki/Convergent_boundary" title="Convergent boundary">convergent boundary</a> (<a href="/wiki/Main_Himalayan_Thrust" title="Main Himalayan Thrust">Main Himalayan Thrust</a>) between the <a href="/wiki/Indo-Australian_Plate" title="Indo-Australian Plate">Indo-Australian Plate</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_Plate" title="Eurasian Plate">Eurasian Plate</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arakan_Yoma" title="Arakan Yoma">Arakan Yoma</a> highlands in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Burma" title="Burma">Myanmar</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Andaman_and_Nicobar_Islands" title="Andaman and Nicobar Islands">Andaman and Nicobar Islands</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Bengal" title="Bay of Bengal">Bay of Bengal</a> were also formed as a result of this collision.
</p><p>During the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Upper_Cretaceous" title="Upper Cretaceous">Upper Cretaceous</a>, about 70 million years ago, the north-moving Indo-Australian Plate (which has subsequently broken into the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Plate" title="Indian Plate">Indian Plate</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Australian_Plate" title="Australian Plate">Australian Plate</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>) was moving at about 15 cm (5.9 in) per year. About 50 million years ago this fast-moving Indo-Australian Plate had completely closed the <a href="/wiki/Tethys_Ocean" title="Tethys Ocean">Tethys Ocean</a>, the existence of which has been determined by <a href="/wiki/Sedimentary_rock" title="Sedimentary rock">sedimentary rocks</a> settled on the ocean floor and the volcanoes that fringed its edges. Since both plates were composed of low density <a href="/wiki/Continental_crust" title="Continental crust">continental crust</a>, they were <a href="/wiki/Thrust_fault" title="Thrust fault">thrust faulted</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fold_(geology)" title="Fold (geology)">folded</a> into mountain ranges rather than subducting into the <a href="/wiki/Mantle_(geology)" title="Mantle (geology)">mantle</a> along an <a href="/wiki/Oceanic_trench" title="Oceanic trench">oceanic trench</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGS_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-USGS-18">[18]</a></sup> An often-cited fact used to illustrate this process is that the summit of Mount Everest is made of marine <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">limestone</a> from this ancient ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>Today, the Indian plate continues to be driven horizontally at the Tibetan Plateau, which forces the plateau to continue to move upwards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> The Indian plate is still moving at 67 mm per year, and over the next 10 million years it will travel about 1,500 km (930 mi) into Asia. About 20 mm per year of the India-Asia convergence is absorbed by <a href="/wiki/Thrust_fault" title="Thrust fault">thrusting</a> along the <a href="/wiki/Main_Frontal_Thrust" title="Main Frontal Thrust">Himalaya southern front</a>. This leads to the Himalayas rising by about 5 mm per year, making them geologically active. The movement of the Indian plate into the Asian plate also makes this region <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seismic" title="Seismic">seismically</a> active, leading to earthquakes from time to time.
</p><p>During the last <a href="/wiki/Ice_age" title="Ice age">ice age</a>, there was a connected ice stream of <a href="/wiki/Glacier" title="Glacier">glaciers</a> between <a href="/wiki/Kangchenjunga" title="Kangchenjunga">Kangchenjunga</a> in the east and <a href="/wiki/Nanga_Parbat" title="Nanga Parbat">Nanga Parbat</a> in the west.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kuhle2011_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kuhle2011-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> In the west, the glaciers joined with the ice stream network in the <a href="/wiki/Karakoram" title="Karakoram">Karakoram</a>, and in the north, they joined with the former Tibetan inland ice. To the south, outflow glaciers came to an end below an elevation of 1,000–2,000 m (3,300–6,600 ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kuhle2011_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kuhle2011-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kuhle1987_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kuhle1987-24">[24]</a></sup> While the current valley glaciers of the Himalaya reach at most 20 to 32 km (12 to 20 mi) in length, several of the main valley glaciers were 60 to 112 km (37 to 70 mi) long during the ice age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kuhle2011_22-2"><a href="#cite_note-Kuhle2011-22">[22]</a></sup> The glacier snowline (the altitude where accumulation and <a href="/wiki/Ablation#Glaciology" title="Ablation">ablation</a> of a glacier are balanced) was about 1,400–1,660 m (4,590–5,450 ft) lower than it is today. Thus, the climate was at least 7.0 to 8.3 °C (12.6 to 14.9 °F) colder than it is today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kuhle2005_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kuhle2005-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Hydrology">Hydrology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Himalayas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Hydrology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{display:flex;flex-direction:row;clear:left;flex-wrap:wrap;width:100%;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{margin:1px;float:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-left{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-right{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-center{text-align:center}@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:none!important;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle .thumbcaption{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow>.thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Confluence_of_Indus_and_Zanskar_rivers.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="5184" decoding="async" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Confluence_of_Indus_and_Zanskar_rivers.jpg/200px-Confluence_of_Indus_and_Zanskar_rivers.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Confluence_of_Indus_and_Zanskar_rivers.jpg/300px-Confluence_of_Indus_and_Zanskar_rivers.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Confluence_of_Indus_and_Zanskar_rivers.jpg/400px-Confluence_of_Indus_and_Zanskar_rivers.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Confluence of the <a href="/wiki/Indus_River" title="Indus River">Indus River</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zanskar_River" title="Zanskar River">Zanskar River</a> in the Himalayas</div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yumthang_valley,_Lachung_Sikkim_India_2012.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Yumthang_valley%2C_Lachung_Sikkim_India_2012.jpg/200px-Yumthang_valley%2C_Lachung_Sikkim_India_2012.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Yumthang_valley%2C_Lachung_Sikkim_India_2012.jpg/300px-Yumthang_valley%2C_Lachung_Sikkim_India_2012.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Yumthang_valley%2C_Lachung_Sikkim_India_2012.jpg/400px-Yumthang_valley%2C_Lachung_Sikkim_India_2012.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yumthang" title="Yumthang">Yumthang</a> river valley in <a href="/wiki/Sikkim" title="Sikkim">Sikkim</a>, India</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>Despite their scale, the Himalayas do not form a major watershed, and a number of rivers cut through the range, particularly in the eastern part of the range. As a result, the main ridge of the Himalayas is not clearly defined, and mountain passes are not as significant for traversing the range as with other mountain ranges. The rivers of the Himalayas drain into two large river systems:
</p>
<ul><li>The western rivers combine into the <i>Indus Basin</i>. The <a href="/wiki/Indus_River" title="Indus River">Indus</a> itself forms the northern and western boundaries of the Himalayas. It begins in Tibet at the confluence of Sengge and Gar rivers and flows north-west through India into Pakistan before turning south-west to the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Sea" title="Arabian Sea">Arabian Sea</a>. It is fed by several major tributaries draining the southern slopes of the Himalayas, including the <a href="/wiki/Jhelum_River" title="Jhelum River">Jhelum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chenab_River" title="Chenab River">Chenab</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ravi_River" title="Ravi River">Ravi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beas_River" title="Beas River">Beas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sutlej" title="Sutlej">Sutlej</a> rivers, the five rivers of the <a href="/wiki/Punjab,_India" title="Punjab, India">Punjab</a>.</li>
<li>The other Himalayan rivers drain the <i>Ganges-Brahmaputra Basin</i>. Its main rivers are the <a href="/wiki/Ganges" title="Ganges">Ganges</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brahmaputra" title="Brahmaputra">Brahmaputra</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Yamuna" title="Yamuna">Yamuna</a>, as well as other tributaries. The Brahmaputra originates as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yarlung_Tsangpo_River_(Tibet)" title="Yarlung Tsangpo River (Tibet)">Yarlung Tsangpo River</a> in western Tibet, and flows east through Tibet and west through the plains of <a href="/wiki/Assam" title="Assam">Assam</a>. The Ganges and the Brahmaputra meet in <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a> and drain into the <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Bengal" title="Bay of Bengal">Bay of Bengal</a> through the world's largest river delta, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunderbans" title="Sunderbans">Sunderbans</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gits4u_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-gits4u-26">[26]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>The northern slopes of <a href="/wiki/Gyala_Peri" title="Gyala Peri">Gyala Peri</a> and the peaks beyond the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yarlung_Tsangpo_River" title="Yarlung Tsangpo River">Tsangpo</a>, sometimes included in the Himalayas, drain into the <a href="/wiki/Irrawaddy_River" title="Irrawaddy River">Irrawaddy River</a>, which originates in eastern Tibet and flows south through Myanmar to drain into the <a href="/wiki/Andaman_Sea" title="Andaman Sea">Andaman Sea</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salween" title="Salween">Salween</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mekong" title="Mekong">Mekong</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yangtze_River" title="Yangtze River">Yangtze</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yellow_River" title="Yellow River">Yellow River</a> all originate from parts of the Tibetan Plateau that are geologically distinct from the Himalaya mountains and are therefore not considered true Himalayan rivers. Some geologists refer to all the rivers collectively as the <i>circum-Himalayan rivers</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-circum_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-circum-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Glaciers">Glaciers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Himalayas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Glaciers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The great ranges of central Asia, including the Himalayas, contain the third-largest deposit of ice and snow in the world, after <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Arctic" title="Arctic">Arctic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pbs_nature_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-pbs_nature-28">[28]</a></sup> The Himalayan range encompasses about 15,000 glaciers, which store about 12,000 km<sup>3</sup> (2,900 cu mi) of fresh water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPPC2007_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-IPPC2007-29">[29]</a></sup> Its glaciers include the <a href="/wiki/Gangotri_Glacier" title="Gangotri Glacier">Gangotri</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yamunotri" title="Yamunotri">Yamunotri</a> (<a href="/wiki/Uttarakhand" title="Uttarakhand">Uttarakhand</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Khumbu_Glacier" title="Khumbu Glacier">Khumbu</a> glaciers (<a href="/wiki/Mount_Everest" title="Mount Everest">Mount Everest</a> region), <a href="/wiki/Langtang" title="Langtang">Langtang</a> glacier (<a href="/wiki/Langtang" title="Langtang">Langtang</a> region) and <a href="/wiki/Zemu_Glacier" title="Zemu Glacier">Zemu</a> (<a href="/wiki/Sikkim" title="Sikkim">Sikkim</a>).
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:KhumbuIcefall.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1163" data-file-width="1772" decoding="async" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/KhumbuIcefall.jpg/220px-KhumbuIcefall.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/KhumbuIcefall.jpg/330px-KhumbuIcefall.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/KhumbuIcefall.jpg/440px-KhumbuIcefall.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:KhumbuIcefall.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Icefall" title="Icefall">Icefall</a> on <a href="/wiki/Khumbu_Glacier" title="Khumbu Glacier">Khumbu Glacier</a></div></div></div>
<p>Owing to the mountains' latitude near the <a href="/wiki/Tropic_of_Cancer" title="Tropic of Cancer">Tropic of Cancer</a>, the permanent <a href="/wiki/Snow_line" title="Snow line">snow line</a> is among the highest in the world at typically around 5,500 m (18,000 ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> In contrast, equatorial mountains in <a href="/wiki/New_Guinea" title="New Guinea">New Guinea</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rwenzoris" title="Rwenzoris">Rwenzoris</a> and <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a> have a snow line some 900 m (2,950 ft) lower.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> The higher regions of the Himalayas are snowbound throughout the year, in spite of their proximity to the tropics, and they form the sources of several large <a href="/wiki/Perennial_stream" title="Perennial stream">perennial rivers</a>.
</p><p>In recent years, scientists have monitored a notable increase in the rate of <a href="/wiki/Retreat_of_glaciers_since_1850" title="Retreat of glaciers since 1850">glacier retreat</a> across the region as a result of climate change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> For example, glacial lakes have been forming rapidly on the surface of debris-covered glaciers in the Bhutan Himalaya during the last few decades. Although the effect of this will not be known for many years, it potentially could mean disaster for the hundreds of millions of people who rely on the glaciers to feed the rivers during the dry seasons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> The global climate change will affect the water resources and livelihoods of the Greater Himalayan region.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lakes">Lakes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Himalayas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Lakes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Alpine_lakes_in_India" title="List of Alpine lakes in India">List of Alpine lakes in India</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gurudongmar_lake_North_Sikkim.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3978" data-file-width="5986" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Gurudongmar_lake_North_Sikkim.jpg/220px-Gurudongmar_lake_North_Sikkim.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Gurudongmar_lake_North_Sikkim.jpg/330px-Gurudongmar_lake_North_Sikkim.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Gurudongmar_lake_North_Sikkim.jpg/440px-Gurudongmar_lake_North_Sikkim.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gurudongmar_lake_North_Sikkim.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Gurudongmar_Lake" title="Gurudongmar Lake">Gurudongmar Lake</a> in Sikkim</div></div></div>
<p>The Himalayan region is dotted with hundreds of lakes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-O'Neill_2019_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-O'Neill_2019-37">[37]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pangong_Tso" title="Pangong Tso">Pangong Tso</a>, which is spread across the border between India and China, at far western end of Tibet, is among the largest with surface areas of 700 km<sup>2</sup> (270 sq mi).
</p><p>South of the main range, the lakes are smaller. <a href="/wiki/Tilicho_Lake" title="Tilicho Lake">Tilicho Lake</a> in Nepal in the Annapurna massif is one of the highest lakes in the world. Other notable lakes include <a href="/wiki/Rara_Lake" title="Rara Lake">Rara Lake</a> in western Nepal, <a href="/wiki/Phoksundo_Lake" title="Phoksundo Lake">She-Phoksundo Lake</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Shey_Phoksundo_National_Park" title="Shey Phoksundo National Park">Shey Phoksundo National Park</a> of Nepal, <a href="/wiki/Gurudongmar_Lake" title="Gurudongmar Lake">Gurudongmar Lake</a>, in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/North_Sikkim" title="North Sikkim">North Sikkim</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gokyo_Lakes" title="Gokyo Lakes">Gokyo Lakes</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solukhumbu" title="Solukhumbu">Solukhumbu</a> district of <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lake_Tsongmo" title="Lake Tsongmo">Lake Tsongmo</a>, near the Indo-China border in Sikkim.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-O'Neill_2019_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-O'Neill_2019-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some of the lakes present a danger of a <a href="/wiki/Glacial_lake_outburst_flood" title="Glacial lake outburst flood">glacial lake outburst flood</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Tsho_Rolpa" title="Tsho Rolpa">Tsho Rolpa</a> glacier lake in the <a href="/wiki/Rolwaling_Himal" title="Rolwaling Himal">Rowaling Valley</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Dolakha_District" title="Dolakha District">Dolakha District</a> of Nepal, is rated as the most dangerous. The lake, which is located at an altitude of 4,580 m (15,030 ft) has grown considerably over the last 50 years due to glacial melting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> The mountain lakes are known to geographers as <i><a href="/wiki/Tarn_(lake)" title="Tarn (lake)">tarns</a></i> if they are caused by glacial activity. Tarns are found mostly in the upper reaches of the Himalaya, above 5,500 m (18,000 ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>Temperate Himalayan wetlands provide important habitat and layover sites for migratory birds. Many mid and low altitude lakes remain poorly studied in terms of their hydrology and biodiversity, like Khecheopalri in the Sikkim Eastern Himalayas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oneill_khecheopalri_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-oneill_khecheopalri-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Himalayas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Temperature">Temperature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Himalayas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Temperature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The physical factors determining the climate in any location in the Himalayas include latitude, altitude, and the relative motion of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southwest_monsoon" title="Southwest monsoon">Southwest monsoon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZurickPacheco200650_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZurickPacheco200650-42">[42]</a></sup> From south to north, the mountains cover more than eight degrees of latitude, spanning temperate to subtropical zones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZurickPacheco200650_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZurickPacheco200650-42">[42]</a></sup>  The colder air of Central Asia is prevented from blowing down into South Asia by the physical configuration of the Himalayas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZurickPacheco200650_42-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZurickPacheco200650-42">[42]</a></sup>  This causes the tropical zone to extend farther north in South Asia than anywhere else in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZurickPacheco200650_42-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZurickPacheco200650-42">[42]</a></sup> The evidence is unmistakeable in the Brahmaputra valley as the warm air from the <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Bengal" title="Bay of Bengal">Bay of Bengal</a> bottlenecks and rushes up past <a href="/wiki/Namcha_Barwa" title="Namcha Barwa">Namcha Barwa</a>, the eastern anchor of the Himalayas, and into southeastern Tibet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZurickPacheco200650_42-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZurickPacheco200650-42">[42]</a></sup> Temperatures in the Himalayas cool by 2.0 degrees C (3.6 degrees F) for every 300 metres (980 ft) increase of altitude.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZurickPacheco200650_42-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZurickPacheco200650-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>As the physical features of mountains are irregular, with broken jagged contours, there can be wide variations in temperature over short distances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZurickPacheco200650–51_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZurickPacheco200650–51-43">[43]</a></sup> Temperature at a location on a mountain depends on the season of the year, the bearing of the sun with respect to the face on which the location lies, and the <a href="/wiki/Mass_versus_weight" title="Mass versus weight">mass</a> of the mountain, i.e. the amount of <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">matter</a> in the mountain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZurickPacheco200650–51_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZurickPacheco200650–51-43">[43]</a></sup>  As the temperature is directly proportional to received radiation from the sun, the faces that receive more direct sunlight also have a greater heat buildup.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZurickPacheco200650–51_43-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZurickPacheco200650–51-43">[43]</a></sup>  In narrow valleys—lying between steep mountain faces—there can be dramatically different weather along their two margins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZurickPacheco200650–51_43-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZurickPacheco200650–51-43">[43]</a></sup> The side to the north with a mountain above facing south can have an extra month of the growing season.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZurickPacheco200650–51_43-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZurickPacheco200650–51-43">[43]</a></sup>  The mass of the mountain also influences the temperature, as it acts as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heat_island" title="Heat island">heat island</a>, in which more heat is absorbed and retained than the surroundings, and therefore influences the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heat_budget" title="Heat budget">heat budget</a> or the amount of heat needed to raise the temperature from the winter minimum winter to the summer maximum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZurickPacheco200650–51_43-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZurickPacheco200650–51-43">[43]</a></sup>
The immense scale of the Himalayas means that many summits can create their own weather, the temperature fluctuating from one summit to another,  from one face to another, and all may be quite different from the weather in nearby plateaus or valleys.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZurickPacheco200650–51_43-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZurickPacheco200650–51-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Precipitation">Precipitation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Himalayas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Precipitation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A critical influence on the Himalayan climate is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southwest_Monsoon" title="Southwest Monsoon">Southwest Monsoon</a>.  This is not so much the rain of the summer months as the wind that carries the rain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZurickPacheco200650–51_43-7"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZurickPacheco200650–51-43">[43]</a></sup> Different rates of heating and cooling between the Central Asian continent and the South Asian ocean create large differences in the <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_pressure" title="Atmospheric pressure">atmospheric pressure</a> prevailing above each.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZurickPacheco200650–51_43-8"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZurickPacheco200650–51-43">[43]</a></sup> In the winter, a high-pressure system forms and remains suspended above Central Asia, forcing air to flow in the southerly direction over the Himalayas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZurickPacheco200650–51_43-9"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZurickPacheco200650–51-43">[43]</a></sup>  But in Central Asia as there is no substantial source for water to be diffused as vapour, the winter winds blowing across South Asia are dry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZurickPacheco200650–51_43-10"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZurickPacheco200650–51-43">[43]</a></sup>  
In the summer months the Central Asian plateau heats up more than the ocean waters to its south.  As a result, the air above it rises higher and higher, creating a zone of low pressure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZurickPacheco200650–51_43-11"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZurickPacheco200650–51-43">[43]</a></sup> Off-shore high-pressure systems in the Indian ocean push the moist summer air inland toward the low-pressure system.  When the moist air meets mountains, it rises and upon subsequent cooling, its moisture condenses and is released as rain, typically heavy rain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZurickPacheco200650–51_43-12"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZurickPacheco200650–51-43">[43]</a></sup>  The wet summer monsoon winds cause precipitation in India and all along the layered southern slopes of the Himalayas.  This forced lifting of air is called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orographic_effect" title="Orographic effect">orographic effect</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZurickPacheco200650–51_43-13"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZurickPacheco200650–51-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Winds">Winds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Himalayas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Winds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The vast size, huge altitude range, and complex topography of the Himalayas mean they experience a wide range of climates, from humid subtropical in the foothills to cold and dry desert conditions on the Tibetan side of the range. For much of the Himalayas—in the areas to the south of the high mountains, the <a href="/wiki/Monsoon" title="Monsoon">monsoon</a> is the most characteristic feature of the climate and causes most of the precipitation, while the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_disturbance" title="Western disturbance">western disturbance</a> brings winter precipitation, especially in the west. Heavy rain arrives on the southwest monsoon in June and persists until September. The monsoon can seriously impact transport and cause major landslides. It restricts tourism – the trekking and mountaineering season is limited to either before the monsoon in April/May or after the monsoon in October/November (autumn). In Nepal and Sikkim, there are often considered to be five seasons: summer, <a href="/wiki/Monsoon" title="Monsoon">monsoon</a>, autumn, (or post-monsoon), winter, and spring.
</p><p>Using the <a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification" title="Köppen climate classification">Köppen climate classification</a>, the lower elevations of the Himalayas, reaching in mid-elevations in central Nepal (including the Kathmandu valley), are classified as <i>Cwa</i>, <a href="/wiki/Humid_subtropical_climate#South_Asia" title="Humid subtropical climate">Humid subtropical climate</a> with dry winters. Higher up, most of the Himalayas have a <a href="/wiki/Oceanic_climate#Subtropical_highland_variety_(Cwb)" title="Oceanic climate">subtropical highland climate (<i>Cwb</i>)</a>.
</p><p>The intensity of the southwest monsoon diminishes as it moves westward along the range, with as much as 2,030 mm (80 in) of rainfall in the monsoon season in <a href="/wiki/Darjeeling" title="Darjeeling">Darjeeling</a> in the east, compared to only 975 mm (38.4 in) during the same period in Shimla in the west.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>The northern side of the Himalayas, also known as the Tibetan Himalaya, is dry, cold and, generally, windswept particularly in the west where it has a <a href="/wiki/Desert_climate#Cold_desert_climates" title="Desert climate">cold desert climate</a>. The vegetation is sparse and stunted and the winters are severely cold. Most of the precipitation in the region is in the form of snow during the late winter and spring months.
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:View_on_Gyakar_(edited).jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="3800" data-file-width="5472" decoding="async" height="139" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/View_on_Gyakar_%28edited%29.jpg/200px-View_on_Gyakar_%28edited%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/View_on_Gyakar_%28edited%29.jpg/300px-View_on_Gyakar_%28edited%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/View_on_Gyakar_%28edited%29.jpg/400px-View_on_Gyakar_%28edited%29.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">The <a href="/wiki/Desert_climate#Cold_desert_climate" title="Desert climate">cold desert</a> region of <a href="/wiki/Upper_Mustang" title="Upper Mustang">Upper Mustang</a>; the region lies to the north of the <a href="/wiki/Annapurna_(mountain_range)" title="Annapurna (mountain range)">Annapurna massif</a> (visible in the background)</div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BhurjungKhola-AK1981.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1500" data-file-width="2250" decoding="async" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/BhurjungKhola-AK1981.jpg/200px-BhurjungKhola-AK1981.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/BhurjungKhola-AK1981.jpg/300px-BhurjungKhola-AK1981.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/BhurjungKhola-AK1981.jpg/400px-BhurjungKhola-AK1981.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">A village in the <a href="/wiki/Pokhara_Valley" title="Pokhara Valley">Pokhara Valley</a> during the monsoon season; the valley lies to the south of the <a href="/wiki/Annapurna_(mountain_range)" title="Annapurna (mountain range)">Annapurna massif</a></div></div></div></div></div>
<p>Local impacts on climate are significant throughout the Himalayas. Temperatures fall by 0.2 to 1.2 °C for every 100 m (330 ft) rise in altitude.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> This gives rise to a variety of climates from a nearly tropical climate in the foothills, to <a href="/wiki/Tundra" title="Tundra">tundra</a> and permanent snow and ice at higher elevations. Local climate is also affected by the topography: The leeward side of the mountains receive less rain while the well exposed slopes get heavy rainfall and the <a href="/wiki/Rain_shadow" title="Rain shadow">rain shadow</a> of large mountains can be significant, for example leading to near desert conditions in the <a href="/wiki/Upper_Mustang" title="Upper Mustang">Upper Mustang</a> which is sheltered from the monsoon rains by the <a href="/wiki/Annapurna" title="Annapurna">Annapurna</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dhaulagiri" title="Dhaulagiri">Dhaulagiri</a> massifs and has annual precipitation of around 300 mm (12 in), while <a href="/wiki/Pokhara" title="Pokhara">Pokhara</a> on the southern side of the massifs has substantial rainfall (3,900 mm or 150 in a year). Thus although annual precipitation is generally higher in east than the west, local variations are often more important.
</p><p>The Himalayas have a profound effect on the climate of the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a> and the Tibetan Plateau. They prevent frigid, dry winds from blowing south into the subcontinent, which keeps South Asia much warmer than corresponding <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Temperate" title="Temperate">temperate</a> regions in the other continents. It also forms a barrier for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monsoon_of_Indian_subcontinent" title="Monsoon of Indian subcontinent">monsoon</a> winds, keeping them from traveling northwards, and causing heavy rainfall in the <a href="/wiki/Terai" title="Terai">Terai</a> region. The Himalayas are also believed to play an important part in the formation of Central Asian deserts, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taklamakan" title="Taklamakan">Taklamakan</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gobi" title="Gobi">Gobi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>An acceleration of <a href="/wiki/Retreat_of_glaciers_since_1850#Himalayas_and_Central_Asia" title="Retreat of glaciers since 1850">ice loss across the Himalayas</a> over the past 40 years has been proven with satellite photos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> 
Even if the ambitious <a href="/wiki/Special_Report_on_Global_Warming_of_1.5_%C2%B0C" title="Special Report on Global Warming of 1.5 °C">1.5 °C target</a> would be reached, the Himalaya glaciers would expectedly lose one third of their surfaces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecology">Ecology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Himalayas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Ecology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ecology_of_the_Himalayas" title="Ecology of the Himalayas">Ecology of the Himalayas</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hemitragus_jemlahicus_Jharal.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="450" data-file-width="700" decoding="async" height="129" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Hemitragus_jemlahicus_Jharal.jpg/200px-Hemitragus_jemlahicus_Jharal.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Hemitragus_jemlahicus_Jharal.jpg/300px-Hemitragus_jemlahicus_Jharal.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Hemitragus_jemlahicus_Jharal.jpg/400px-Hemitragus_jemlahicus_Jharal.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Male <a href="/wiki/Himalayan_tahr" title="Himalayan tahr">Himalayan tahr</a></div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RedPandaFullBody.JPG"><img alt="" data-file-height="2104" data-file-width="2805" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/RedPandaFullBody.JPG/200px-RedPandaFullBody.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/RedPandaFullBody.JPG/300px-RedPandaFullBody.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/RedPandaFullBody.JPG/400px-RedPandaFullBody.JPG 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><a href="/wiki/Red_panda" title="Red panda">Red panda</a></div></div></div></div></div>
<p>The flora and fauna of the Himalayas vary with climate, rainfall, altitude, and soils. The climate ranges from tropical at the base of the mountains to permanent ice and snow at the highest elevations. The amount of yearly rainfall increases from west to east along the southern front of the range. This diversity of altitude, rainfall, and soil conditions combined with the very high snow line supports a variety of distinct plant and animal communities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-O'Neill_2019_37-2"><a href="#cite_note-O'Neill_2019-37">[37]</a></sup> The extremes of high altitude (low atmospheric pressure) combined with extreme cold favor <a href="/wiki/Extremophile" title="Extremophile">extremophile</a> organisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oneill_khecheopalri_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-oneill_khecheopalri-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>At high altitudes, the elusive and previously endangered <a href="/wiki/Snow_leopard" title="Snow leopard">snow leopard</a> is the main predator. Its prey includes members of the goat family grazing on the alpine pastures and living on the rocky terrain, notably the endemic <a href="/wiki/Bharal" title="Bharal">bharal</a> or Himalayan blue sheep. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Himalayan_musk_deer" title="Himalayan musk deer">Himalayan musk deer</a> is also found at high altitudes. Hunted for its musk, it is now rare and endangered. Other endemic or near-endemic herbivores include the <a href="/wiki/Himalayan_tahr" title="Himalayan tahr">Himalayan tahr</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Takin" title="Takin">takin</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Himalayan_serow" title="Himalayan serow">Himalayan serow</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Himalayan_goral" title="Himalayan goral">Himalayan goral</a>. The critically endangered <a href="/wiki/Himalayan_brown_bear" title="Himalayan brown bear">Himalayan</a> subspecies of the <a href="/wiki/Brown_bear" title="Brown bear">brown bear</a> is found sporadically across the range as is the <a href="/wiki/Asian_black_bear" title="Asian black bear">Asian black bear</a>. In the mountainous mixed deciduous and conifer forests of the eastern Himalayas, <a href="/wiki/Red_panda" title="Red panda">Red panda</a> feed in the dense understories of bamboo. Lower down the forests of the foothills are inhabited by several different primates, including the endangered <a href="/wiki/Gee%27s_golden_langur" title="Gee's golden langur">Gee's golden langur</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Kashmir_gray_langur" title="Kashmir gray langur">Kashmir gray langur</a>, with highly restricted ranges in the east and west of the Himalayas respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oneill_khecheopalri_41-2"><a href="#cite_note-oneill_khecheopalri-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>The unique floral and faunal wealth of the Himalayas is undergoing structural and compositional changes due to <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/Hydrangea_hirta" title="Hydrangea hirta">Hydrangea hirta</a></i> is an example of floral species that can be found in this area. The increase in temperature is shifting various species to higher elevations. The oak forest is being invaded by pine forests in the Garhwal Himalayan region. There are reports of early flowering and fruiting in some tree species, especially <a href="/wiki/Rhododendron" title="Rhododendron">rhododendron</a>, apple and <i><a href="/wiki/Myrica_esculenta" title="Myrica esculenta">box myrtle</a></i>. The highest known tree species in the Himalayas is <i><a href="/wiki/Juniperus_tibetica" title="Juniperus tibetica">Juniperus tibetica</a></i> located at 4,900 m (16,080 ft) in Southeastern Tibet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Religions">Religions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Himalayas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Religions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Taktshang.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Taktshang.jpg/200px-Taktshang.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Taktshang.jpg/300px-Taktshang.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Taktshang.jpg/400px-Taktshang.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">The <a href="/wiki/Paro_Taktsang" title="Paro Taktsang">Taktsang</a> Monastery, <a href="/wiki/Bhutan" title="Bhutan">Bhutan</a>, also known as the "Tiger's Nest"</div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Harmukh_Mountain.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="4608" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Harmukh_Mountain.jpg/200px-Harmukh_Mountain.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Harmukh_Mountain.jpg/300px-Harmukh_Mountain.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Harmukh_Mountain.jpg/400px-Harmukh_Mountain.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><a href="/wiki/Harmukh" title="Harmukh">Harmukh</a> is a sacred mountain for Hindus located in the Himalayas north of <a href="/wiki/Kashmir_Valley" title="Kashmir Valley">Kashmir Valley</a>.</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>There are many cultural and mythological aspects associated with the Himalayas. In <a href="/wiki/Jainism" title="Jainism">Jainism</a>, Mount Ashtapad of the Himalayan mountain range, is a sacred place where the first Jain <a href="/wiki/Tirthankara" title="Tirthankara">Tirthankara</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rishabhanatha" title="Rishabhanatha">Rishabhdeva</a> attained <a href="/wiki/Moksha" title="Moksha">moksha</a>. It is believed that after <a href="/wiki/Rishabhanatha" title="Rishabhanatha">Rishabhdeva</a> attained <i><a href="/wiki/Nirvana" title="Nirvana">nirvana</a></i>, his son, Emperor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bharata_Chakravartin" title="Bharata Chakravartin">Bharata Chakravartin</a>, had constructed three stupas and twenty four shrines of the 24 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tirthankaras" title="Tirthankaras">Tirthankaras</a> with their idols studded with precious stones over there and named it <i>Sinhnishdha</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> For the Hindus, the Himalayas are personified as <a href="/wiki/Himavat" title="Himavat">Himavat</a>, king of all mountains and the father of the goddess <a href="/wiki/Parvati" title="Parvati">Parvati</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-57">[57]</a></sup> The Himalayas are also considered to be the father of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ganga" title="Ganga">Ganga</a> (the personification of river Ganges).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> Two of the most sacred places of pilgrimage for the Hindus are the temple complex in <a href="/wiki/Pashupatinath_Temple" title="Pashupatinath Temple">Pashupatinath</a> and <a href="/wiki/Muktinath" title="Muktinath">Muktinath</a>, also known as Saligrama because of the presence of the sacred black rocks called saligrams.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JEE_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-JEE-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddhists" title="Buddhists">Buddhists</a> also lay a great deal of importance on the Himalayas. Paro Taktsang is the holy place where <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> started in <a href="/wiki/Bhutan" title="Bhutan">Bhutan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> The Muktinath is also a place of pilgrimage for the Tibetan Buddhists. They believe that the trees in the poplar grove came from the walking sticks of eighty-four ancient Indian Buddhist magicians or <i>mahasiddhas</i>. They consider the saligrams to be representatives of the Tibetan serpent deity known as Gawo Jagpa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-61">[61]</a></sup>
The Himalayan people's diversity shows in many different ways. It shows through their architecture, their languages, and dialects, their beliefs and rituals, as well as their clothing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-:4-61">[61]</a></sup> The shapes and materials of the people's homes reflect their practical needs and beliefs. Another example of the diversity amongst the Himalayan peoples is that handwoven textiles display colors and patterns unique to their ethnic backgrounds. Finally, some people place great importance on jewelry. The Rai and Limbu women wear big gold earrings and nose rings to show their wealth through their jewelry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_61-2"><a href="#cite_note-:4-61">[61]</a></sup>
Several places in the Himalayas are of religious significance in <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jainism" title="Jainism">Jainism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sikhism" title="Sikhism">Sikhism</a>. A notable example of a religious site is <a href="/wiki/Paro_Taktsang" title="Paro Taktsang">Paro Taktsang</a>, where <a href="/wiki/Padmasambhava" title="Padmasambhava">Padmasambhava</a> is said to have founded <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> in <a href="/wiki/Bhutan" title="Bhutan">Bhutan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pommaret_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pommaret-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>A number of <a href="/wiki/Vajrayana" title="Vajrayana">Vajrayana Buddhist</a> sites are situated in the Himalayas, in <a href="/wiki/Tibet" title="Tibet">Tibet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bhutan" title="Bhutan">Bhutan</a> and in the Indian regions of <a href="/wiki/Ladakh" title="Ladakh">Ladakh</a>, Sikkim, <a href="/wiki/Arunachal_Pradesh" title="Arunachal Pradesh">Arunachal Pradesh</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spiti_Valley" title="Spiti Valley">Spiti</a> and <a href="/wiki/Darjeeling" title="Darjeeling">Darjeeling</a>. There were over 6,000 <a href="/wiki/List_of_Tibetan_monasteries" title="List of Tibetan monasteries">monasteries</a> in Tibet, including the residence of the <a href="/wiki/Dalai_Lama" title="Dalai Lama">Dalai Lama</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bhutan" title="Bhutan">Bhutan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sikkim" title="Sikkim">Sikkim</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ladakh" title="Ladakh">Ladakh</a> are also dotted with numerous monasteries.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Resources">Resources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Himalayas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Resources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The Himalayas are home to a diversity of medicinal resources. Plants from the forests have been used for millennia to treat conditions ranging from simple coughs to snake bites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JEE_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-JEE-59">[59]</a></sup> Different parts of the plants – root, flower, stem, leaves, and bark – are used as remedies for different ailments. For example, a bark extract from an <i><a href="/wiki/Abies_pindrow" title="Abies pindrow">Abies pindrow</a></i> tree is used to treat coughs and bronchitis. Leaf and stem paste from an <i><a href="/wiki/Andrachne" title="Andrachne">Andrachne cordifolia</a></i> is used for wounds and as an antidote for snake bites. The bark of a <i><a href="/wiki/Callicarpa" title="Callicarpa">Callicarpa arborea</a></i> is used for skin ailments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JEE_59-2"><a href="#cite_note-JEE-59">[59]</a></sup> Nearly a fifth of the <a href="/wiki/Gymnosperm" title="Gymnosperm">gymnosperms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Flowering_plant" title="Flowering plant">angiosperms</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pteridophyte" title="Pteridophyte">pteridophytes</a> in the Himalayas are found to have medicinal properties, and more are likely to be discovered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JEE_59-3"><a href="#cite_note-JEE-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most of the population in some Asian and African countries depends on medicinal plants rather than prescriptions and such.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-57">[57]</a></sup> Since so many people use medicinal plants as their only source of healing in the Himalayas, the plants are an important source of income. This contributes to economic and modern industrial development both inside and outside the region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_57-2"><a href="#cite_note-:1-57">[57]</a></sup> The only problem is that locals are rapidly clearing the forests on the Himalayas for wood, often illegally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p>

<li>Ives, Jack D. and Messerli, Bruno, <i>The Himalayan Dilemma: Reconciling Development and Conservation</i>. London / New York, Routledge, 1989. <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-415-01157-4" title="Special:BookSources/0-415-01157-4">0-415-01157-4</a></li>
<li>Lall, J.S. (ed.) in association with Moddie, A.D., <i>The Himalaya, Aspects of Change</i>. Delhi, Oxford University Press, 1981. <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-19-561254-X" title="Special:BookSources/0-19-561254-X">0-19-561254-X</a></li>
<li>Nandy, S.N., Dhyani, P.P. and Samal, P.K., <i><a class="external text" href="http://gbpihedenvis.nic.in/ENVIS%20Monograph/ENVIS%20Monograph%203.pdf" rel="nofollow">Resource Information Database of the Indian Himalaya</a></i>, Almora, GBPIHED, 2006.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Swami_Sundaranand" title="Swami Sundaranand">Swami Sundaranand</a>, <i>Himalaya: Through the Lens of a Sadhu</i>. Published by Tapovan Kuti Prakashan (2001). <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/81-901326-0-1" title="Special:BookSources/81-901326-0-1">81-901326-0-1</a></li>

<li><a href="/wiki/Bill_Tilman" title="Bill Tilman">Tilman, H. W.</a>, <i>Mount Everest, 1938</i>, Cambridge University Press, 1948.</li>
